## Chapter 3: Scattering

### Introduction

Scattering, a fundamental concept in the study of atmospheric radiation, is the focus of this chapter. It is a process that plays a crucial role in the interaction of light with the Earth's atmosphere, influencing everything from the color of the sky to the amount of solar radiation reaching the surface. 

In this chapter, we will delve into the intricacies of scattering, exploring its various types, mechanisms, and implications. We will begin by defining scattering, explaining its physical meaning and its importance in atmospheric science. We will then explore the different types of scattering, including Rayleigh scattering, Mie scattering, and Raman scattering, each of which has its unique characteristics and applications.

We will also discuss the mathematical models used to describe scattering, such as the scattering matrix and the scattering cross-section. These models, while complex, are essential tools for understanding and predicting the behavior of light in the atmosphere. For instance, the scattering matrix, denoted as $S$, is a 3x3 matrix that describes the transformation of the Stokes vector $I$ under scattering, as represented by the equation $I' = SI$.

Finally, we will discuss the practical applications of scattering in atmospheric science, such as its role in the formation of rainbows and its impact on the Earth's climate. By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of scattering and its role in atmospheric radiation.




#### 2.3b Band Models in Atmospheric Radiation

Band models are particularly useful in the study of atmospheric radiation, as they allow us to consider the absorption and emission of radiation by multiple atmospheric gases simultaneously. In this section, we will discuss the application of band models in atmospheric radiation, with a focus on the non-radiative dielectric waveguide.

The non-radiative dielectric waveguide is a mathematical model used to describe the propagation of electromagnetic waves in a dielectric medium. In the context of atmospheric radiation, this model can be used to describe the propagation of radiation through the atmosphere. The model is defined by the following equations:

$$
T_{o-}^{TE}=H cos(\frac{m\pi }{a}y)e^{jk_{xo}(x-w)} \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ (25)
$$

$$
k_{xo}=k_{o}\sqrt{1-(\frac{m\pi }{k_{o}a})^{2}-(\frac{k_{z}}{k_{o}})^{2}} \ \ \ \ \ \ \ (26)
$$

In these equations, $T_{o-}^{TE}$ is the transverse electric (TE) mode of the waveguide, $H$ is the height of the waveguide, $m$ is the mode number, $a$ is the width of the waveguide, $k_{xo}$ is the propagation constant, $k_{o}$ is the wave number, and $k_{z}$ is the wave number in the z-direction.

The constants $A$, $B$, $C$, $D$, $E$, $F$, $G$, and $H$ in these equations are determined by imposing the following continuity conditions for the tangential components $E_{y}$, $E_{z}$, $H_{y}$, and $H_{z}$ of the electromagnetic field at $x = w$ and at $x = -w$:

$$
-\frac{k_{z}}{\omega \varepsilon _{o}}\frac{\partial T_{o}}{\partial y}^{TM}+\frac{\partial T_{o}}{\partial x}^{TE}= -\frac{k_{z}}{\omega \varepsilon _{o}\varepsilon _{r}}\frac{\partial T_{\varepsilon } }{\partial y}^{TM}+\frac{\partial T_{\varepsilon }}{\partial x}^{TE}
$$

$$
\frac{k_{o}^{2}-k_{z}^{2}}{j\omega \varepsilon _{o}} \ T_{o}^{TM} =\frac{k_{o}^{2}\varepsilon _{r}-k_{z}^{2}}{j\omega \varepsilon _{r}\varepsilon _{o}} T_{\varepsilon }^{TM}
$$

$$
-\frac{\partial T_{o}}{\partial x}^{TM}-\frac{k_{z}}{\omega \mu }\frac{\partial T_{o}}{\partial y}^{TE}= -\frac{\partial T_{\varepsilon }}{\partial x}^{TM}-\frac{k_{z}}{\omega \mu }\frac{\partial T_{\varepsilon } }{\partial y}^{TE}
$$

$$
\frac{k_{o}^{2}-k_{z}^{2}}{j\omega \mu} \ T_{o}^{TE} =\frac{k_{o}^{2}\varepsilon _{r}-k_{z}^{2}}{j\omega \mu }^{TE}
$$

These continuity conditions allow us to solve for the constants $A$, $B$, $C$, $D$, $E$, $F$, $G$, and $H$ in terms of the propagation constant $k_{xo}$ and the mode number $m$.

The non-radiative dielectric waveguide is a powerful tool for understanding the propagation of radiation through the atmosphere. By incorporating band models into this framework, we can further enhance our understanding of atmospheric radiation and its interaction with atmospheric gases.

#### 2.3c Applications of Band Models in Atmospheric Radiation

Band models have a wide range of applications in the study of atmospheric radiation. They are particularly useful in understanding the absorption and emission of radiation by atmospheric gases, as well as in predicting the effects of these processes on the Earth's climate.

One of the key applications of band models in atmospheric radiation is in the study of the non-radiative dielectric waveguide. As we have seen in the previous section, the non-radiative dielectric waveguide is a mathematical model used to describe the propagation of electromagnetic waves in a dielectric medium. In the context of atmospheric radiation, this model can be used to describe the propagation of radiation through the atmosphere.

The band models are used to represent the absorption and emission of radiation by atmospheric gases in the non-radiative dielectric waveguide. The absorption and emission of radiation by atmospheric gases can be represented as a sum of contributions from individual bands. This allows us to model the effects of atmospheric gases on the transmission of radiation with a relatively small number of parameters.

The band models are also used in the development of atmospheric radiative transfer models, such as the two-stream approximation and the discrete ordinate method. These models use band models to represent the absorption and emission of radiation by atmospheric gases, and they are used to predict the effects of these processes on the Earth's climate.

In the context of the non-radiative dielectric waveguide, the band models are used to solve for the constants $A$, $B$, $C$, $D$, $E$, $F$, $G$, and $H$ in terms of the propagation constant $k_{xo}$ and the mode number $m$. This allows us to understand the propagation of radiation through the atmosphere in a more detailed manner.

In conclusion, band models play a crucial role in the study of atmospheric radiation. They allow us to understand the absorption and emission of radiation by atmospheric gases, and to predict the effects of these processes on the Earth's climate. Their application in the non-radiative dielectric waveguide provides a powerful tool for understanding the propagation of radiation through the atmosphere.




### Subsection: 2.3c Limitations of Band Models

While band models, such as the non-radiative dielectric waveguide, are powerful tools for understanding the propagation of radiation through the atmosphere, they do have some limitations. These limitations are primarily due to the simplifications and assumptions made in the models.

One of the main limitations of band models is their reliance on linear approximations. In reality, the atmosphere is a complex, non-linear system, and the propagation of radiation through it is influenced by a multitude of factors. Linear approximations, while useful for simplifying the problem, can lead to significant errors in the predicted behavior of the system.

Another limitation of band models is their inability to account for all atmospheric gases. The non-radiative dielectric waveguide, for example, only considers the propagation of radiation through a dielectric medium. This means that it cannot accurately predict the behavior of radiation in the presence of gases that are not dielectric, such as water vapor or carbon dioxide.

Furthermore, band models often rely on empirical data for their parameters. This data is often collected under specific conditions and may not be applicable to all situations. For example, the constants $A$, $B$, $C$, $D$, $E$, $F$, $G$, and $H$ in the non-radiative dielectric waveguide are determined from empirical data. If this data is not representative of the conditions under which the model is being used, the predictions of the model may be inaccurate.

Finally, band models often assume that the atmosphere is homogeneous and isotropic. In reality, the atmosphere is highly heterogeneous and anisotropic, with variations in temperature, pressure, and composition that can significantly affect the propagation of radiation.

Despite these limitations, band models remain valuable tools for understanding the behavior of radiation in the atmosphere. They provide a simplified, yet powerful, means of studying complex phenomena. However, it is important to be aware of these limitations and to use the models appropriately, taking into account their assumptions and simplifications.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the intricate world of atmospheric radiation, focusing on the fundamental concepts of absorption and emission. We have explored how these processes are integral to the balance of energy in the atmosphere, and how they contribute to the overall climate of our planet. 

We have learned that absorption is the process by which atmospheric gases absorb incoming solar radiation, converting it into heat. This heat is then redistributed throughout the atmosphere, influencing weather patterns and climate. On the other hand, emission is the process by which the atmosphere radiates heat back into space. This balance between absorption and emission is crucial to maintaining the Earth's temperature and climate.

Furthermore, we have examined the mathematical models that describe these processes, providing a quantitative understanding of atmospheric radiation. These models, while complex, offer a powerful tool for predicting and understanding the behavior of our atmosphere.

In conclusion, the study of atmospheric radiation is a vast and complex field, but one that is essential to our understanding of our planet's climate and weather patterns. By studying absorption and emission, we can gain a deeper understanding of the processes that shape our world.

## Chapter: Molecular Absorption

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 3: Molecular Absorption. This chapter delves into the fascinating world of molecular absorption, a fundamental process in atmospheric radiation. Molecular absorption is a key mechanism by which molecules in the atmosphere interact with electromagnetic radiation, such as sunlight. This interaction is crucial for a variety of atmospheric phenomena, including the formation of the ozone layer, the greenhouse effect, and the color of the sky.

In this chapter, we will explore the principles of molecular absorption, starting with the basic concepts and gradually moving on to more complex topics. We will discuss the absorption spectrum of molecules, which is a unique fingerprint that identifies a particular molecule. We will also delve into the quantum mechanical basis of molecular absorption, explaining how molecules absorb specific wavelengths of light.

We will also explore the role of molecular absorption in atmospheric processes. For instance, we will discuss how molecular absorption contributes to the greenhouse effect, a process that warms the Earth's surface and makes life possible. We will also look at how molecular absorption is responsible for the color of the sky, a phenomenon that has intrigued scientists and philosophers for centuries.

Throughout this chapter, we will use mathematical equations to describe the processes of molecular absorption. For example, we might use the equation `$\Delta w = ...$` to represent the change in molecular absorption due to a change in atmospheric conditions. We will also use diagrams and illustrations to help you visualize these concepts.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of molecular absorption and its role in atmospheric radiation. You should be able to explain the principles of molecular absorption, understand the absorption spectrum of molecules, and appreciate the role of molecular absorption in atmospheric processes.

So, let's embark on this journey into the world of molecular absorption, where science meets beauty and complexity meets simplicity.




### Subsection: 2.4a Introduction to MODTRAN

The Moderate Resolution Imaging Spectroradiometer (MODIS) is a powerful tool for studying the Earth's atmosphere. It is a sensor on the NASA's Terra and Aqua satellites, and it collects data on the Earth's surface, atmosphere, and clouds. The MODIS Data Processing System (MDPS) is responsible for processing the raw data collected by MODIS into usable products.

The MODTRAN (MODIS Transmission) algorithm is a key component of the MDPS. It is used to calculate the atmospheric transmission for each MODIS spectral band. This is done by solving the radiative transfer equation for each band, taking into account the atmospheric conditions at the time and location of the observation.

The MODTRAN algorithm is based on the two-stream approximation, which assumes that the atmosphere can be represented by two streams of radiation: one stream that is transmitted through the atmosphere, and one stream that is scattered back into space. This approximation is valid for many atmospheric conditions, but it may not be accurate for situations where the atmosphere is highly non-uniform or where there are strong scattering or absorbing particles present.

The MODTRAN algorithm also takes into account the effects of aerosols on the atmospheric transmission. Aerosols are small particles suspended in the atmosphere, and they can significantly affect the amount of radiation that is transmitted through the atmosphere. The MODTRAN algorithm uses a lookup table to account for the effects of aerosols, which is based on measurements of the aerosol optical depth.

The MODTRAN algorithm is implemented in the MODTRAN4 code, which is written in Fortran 95. The code is structured into several modules, each of which performs a specific task. These modules are:

- `atm_trans.f`: This module calculates the atmospheric transmission for each MODIS spectral band.
- `aerosol.f`: This module accounts for the effects of aerosols on the atmospheric transmission.
- `cloud.f`: This module accounts for the effects of clouds on the atmospheric transmission.
- `surface.f`: This module accounts for the effects of the Earth's surface on the atmospheric transmission.
- `rad_trans.f`: This module solves the radiative transfer equation for each MODIS spectral band.
- `lookup.f`: This module uses a lookup table to account for the effects of aerosols on the atmospheric transmission.
- `main.f`: This module is the main driver of the MODTRAN4 code.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the MODTRAN algorithm and explore how it is used to calculate the atmospheric transmission for each MODIS spectral band.

### Subsection: 2.4b MODTRAN Algorithm

The MODTRAN algorithm is a complex process that involves several steps to calculate the atmospheric transmission for each MODIS spectral band. The algorithm is based on the two-stream approximation, which assumes that the atmosphere can be represented by two streams of radiation: one stream that is transmitted through the atmosphere, and one stream that is scattered back into space. This approximation is valid for many atmospheric conditions, but it may not be accurate for situations where the atmosphere is highly non-uniform or where there are strong scattering or absorbing particles present.

The MODTRAN algorithm begins by calculating the atmospheric transmission for each spectral band. This is done by solving the radiative transfer equation for each band, taking into account the atmospheric conditions at the time and location of the observation. The radiative transfer equation is a mathematical representation of the physical processes that govern the propagation of radiation through the atmosphere. It takes into account the absorption, emission, and scattering of radiation by atmospheric gases, particles, and clouds.

The MODTRAN algorithm then accounts for the effects of aerosols on the atmospheric transmission. Aerosols are small particles suspended in the atmosphere, and they can significantly affect the amount of radiation that is transmitted through the atmosphere. The MODTRAN algorithm uses a lookup table to account for the effects of aerosols, which is based on measurements of the aerosol optical depth. The aerosol optical depth is a measure of the amount of light that is scattered or absorbed by aerosols in the atmosphere.

Next, the MODTRAN algorithm accounts for the effects of clouds on the atmospheric transmission. Clouds can significantly reduce the amount of radiation that reaches the Earth's surface, and they can also affect the amount of radiation that is transmitted through the atmosphere. The MODTRAN algorithm uses a lookup table to account for the effects of clouds, which is based on measurements of the cloud optical depth. The cloud optical depth is a measure of the amount of light that is scattered or absorbed by clouds in the atmosphere.

Finally, the MODTRAN algorithm accounts for the effects of the Earth's surface on the atmospheric transmission. The Earth's surface can absorb or reflect radiation, depending on its albedo. The MODTRAN algorithm uses a lookup table to account for the effects of the Earth's surface, which is based on measurements of the surface albedo.

The MODTRAN algorithm is implemented in the MODTRAN4 code, which is written in Fortran 95. The code is structured into several modules, each of which performs a specific task. These modules are:

- `atm_trans.f`: This module calculates the atmospheric transmission for each MODIS spectral band.
- `aerosol.f`: This module accounts for the effects of aerosols on the atmospheric transmission.
- `cloud.f`: This module accounts for the effects of clouds on the atmospheric transmission.
- `surface.f`: This module accounts for the effects of the Earth's surface on the atmospheric transmission.
- `rad_trans.f`: This module solves the radiative transfer equation for each MODIS spectral band.
- `lookup.f`: This module uses a lookup table to account for the effects of aerosols, clouds, and the Earth's surface on the atmospheric transmission.
- `main.f`: This module is the main driver of the MODTRAN4 code.

### Subsection: 2.4c Applications of MODTRAN

The MODTRAN algorithm, with its ability to accurately calculate atmospheric transmission, has a wide range of applications in atmospheric science and remote sensing. This section will explore some of these applications in detail.

#### 2.4c.1 Atmospheric Science

In atmospheric science, MODTRAN is used to study the effects of various atmospheric conditions on the transmission of radiation. This includes the study of the effects of different atmospheric gases, particles, and clouds on the transmission of radiation. By using MODTRAN, scientists can simulate the transmission of radiation under different atmospheric conditions, allowing them to better understand the physical processes that govern the propagation of radiation through the atmosphere.

#### 2.4c.2 Remote Sensing

MODTRAN is also used in remote sensing, which is the process of collecting information about the Earth's atmosphere and surface from a distance. Remote sensing is used in a variety of applications, including weather forecasting, climate research, and environmental monitoring. MODTRAN is used in remote sensing to calculate the amount of radiation that is transmitted through the atmosphere, which can then be used to infer information about the Earth's atmosphere and surface.

#### 2.4c.3 Climate Research

In climate research, MODTRAN is used to study the effects of changes in the Earth's atmosphere on the transmission of radiation. This includes the study of the effects of changes in the concentration of atmospheric gases, particles, and clouds on the transmission of radiation. By using MODTRAN, scientists can simulate the transmission of radiation under different climate scenarios, allowing them to better understand the potential impacts of climate change on the Earth's atmosphere and surface.

#### 2.4c.4 Space Science

In space science, MODTRAN is used to study the effects of the Earth's atmosphere on the transmission of radiation from space. This includes the study of the effects of the Earth's atmosphere on the transmission of radiation from stars and other celestial bodies. By using MODTRAN, scientists can simulate the transmission of radiation from space under different atmospheric conditions, allowing them to better understand the physical processes that govern the propagation of radiation through the Earth's atmosphere.

In conclusion, the MODTRAN algorithm, with its ability to accurately calculate atmospheric transmission, has a wide range of applications in atmospheric science and remote sensing. By using MODTRAN, scientists can gain a deeper understanding of the physical processes that govern the propagation of radiation through the Earth's atmosphere, leading to advancements in our understanding of the Earth's atmosphere and surface.

## Chapter: Molecular Absorption and Emission

### Introduction

The third chapter of "Atmospheric Radiation: A Comprehensive Guide" delves into the fascinating world of Molecular Absorption and Emission. This chapter is designed to provide a comprehensive understanding of the fundamental principles that govern the absorption and emission of radiation by molecules in the atmosphere.

Molecular absorption and emission are two critical processes that play a significant role in the Earth's atmosphere. They are responsible for the absorption and emission of electromagnetic radiation, which are fundamental to many atmospheric phenomena such as the formation of clouds, the greenhouse effect, and the color of the sky.

In this chapter, we will explore the quantum mechanical basis of molecular absorption and emission. We will delve into the concept of energy levels and transitions, and how these processes are influenced by the properties of the molecules and the incident radiation. We will also discuss the practical implications of these processes in atmospheric science.

We will also explore the mathematical models that describe these processes. These models, while complex, provide a powerful tool for predicting and understanding atmospheric phenomena. For instance, the Beer-Lambert Law, represented as `$A = \epsilon \cdot c \cdot l$`, where `$A$` is the absorbance, `$\epsilon$` is the molar absorptivity, `$c$` is the concentration of the absorbing species, and `$l$` is the path length of the radiation through the absorbing medium, is a fundamental equation in this field.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of the principles of molecular absorption and emission, and be able to apply these principles to understand and predict atmospheric phenomena.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of molecular absorption and emission, bridging the gap between theoretical principles and practical applications. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field of atmospheric science, this chapter will serve as a valuable resource in your journey to understand the complex world of atmospheric radiation.




### Subsection: 2.4b Using MODTRAN for Atmospheric Analysis

The MODTRAN algorithm is a powerful tool for studying the Earth's atmosphere. It allows us to calculate the atmospheric transmission for each MODIS spectral band, taking into account the atmospheric conditions at the time and location of the observation. This information can be used to study the effects of atmospheric particles, such as aerosols and water vapor, on the Earth's radiation budget.

#### 2.4b.1 MODTRAN for Atmospheric Analysis

The MODTRAN algorithm can be used to perform atmospheric analysis by calculating the atmospheric transmission for each MODIS spectral band. This can be done for a specific location and time, or for a range of locations and times. The results can then be compared to observations to study the effects of atmospheric particles on the Earth's radiation budget.

For example, the MODTRAN algorithm can be used to study the effects of aerosols on the Earth's radiation budget. Aerosols are small particles suspended in the atmosphere, and they can significantly affect the amount of radiation that is transmitted through the atmosphere. The MODTRAN algorithm uses a lookup table to account for the effects of aerosols, which is based on measurements of the aerosol optical depth. By calculating the atmospheric transmission for different levels of aerosol optical depth, we can study the effects of aerosols on the Earth's radiation budget.

#### 2.4b.2 MODTRAN for Climate Studies

The MODTRAN algorithm is also used in climate studies. By calculating the atmospheric transmission for different atmospheric conditions, we can study the effects of atmospheric particles on the Earth's radiation budget over time. This can help us understand how changes in the Earth's atmosphere are affecting the Earth's climate.

For example, the MODTRAN algorithm can be used to study the effects of greenhouse gases on the Earth's radiation budget. Greenhouse gases, such as carbon dioxide and methane, absorb and re-emit infrared radiation, which can affect the amount of radiation that is transmitted through the atmosphere. By calculating the atmospheric transmission for different levels of greenhouse gases, we can study the effects of these gases on the Earth's radiation budget.

#### 2.4b.3 MODTRAN for Atmospheric Modeling

The MODTRAN algorithm is also used in atmospheric modeling. By calculating the atmospheric transmission for different atmospheric conditions, we can develop models that can predict the effects of atmospheric particles on the Earth's radiation budget. These models can be used to study the effects of different atmospheric conditions on the Earth's climate, and to predict future changes in the Earth's climate.

For example, the MODTRAN algorithm can be used to develop a model that predicts the effects of aerosols on the Earth's radiation budget. By calculating the atmospheric transmission for different levels of aerosol optical depth, we can develop a model that can predict how changes in aerosol levels will affect the Earth's radiation budget. This model can then be used to study the effects of aerosols on the Earth's climate, and to predict future changes in the Earth's climate due to changes in aerosol levels.




### Subsection: 2.4c Case Studies

In this section, we will explore some case studies that demonstrate the use of MODTRAN in atmospheric analysis and climate studies. These case studies will provide a deeper understanding of the concepts discussed in the previous sections.

#### 2.4c.1 Case Study 1: The Effects of Aerosols on the Earth's Radiation Budget

In this case study, we will use MODTRAN to study the effects of aerosols on the Earth's radiation budget. We will focus on the Sahara desert, known for its high levels of aerosols due to the prevalence of dust storms.

Using MODTRAN, we can calculate the atmospheric transmission for different levels of aerosol optical depth in the Sahara desert. We can then compare these results to observations to understand how aerosols are affecting the Earth's radiation budget in this region.

#### 2.4c.2 Case Study 2: The Role of Greenhouse Gases in Climate Change

In this case study, we will use MODTRAN to study the role of greenhouse gases in climate change. We will focus on the Arctic region, where the effects of greenhouse gases are particularly pronounced.

Using MODTRAN, we can calculate the atmospheric transmission for different levels of greenhouse gases in the Arctic region. We can then compare these results to observations to understand how greenhouse gases are affecting the Earth's radiation budget in this region.

#### 2.4c.3 Case Study 3: The Impact of Volcanic Eruptions on the Earth's Radiation Budget

In this case study, we will use MODTRAN to study the impact of volcanic eruptions on the Earth's radiation budget. We will focus on the eruption of Mount Pinatubo in 1991, which released large amounts of sulfur dioxide into the atmosphere.

Using MODTRAN, we can calculate the atmospheric transmission for different levels of sulfur dioxide in the atmosphere. We can then compare these results to observations to understand how volcanic eruptions are affecting the Earth's radiation budget.

These case studies demonstrate the power of MODTRAN in atmospheric analysis and climate studies. By using MODTRAN, we can gain a deeper understanding of the complex interactions between the Earth's atmosphere and radiation.




### Subsection: 2.5a Introduction to Discrete Ordinates Method

The Discrete Ordinates Method (DOM) is a numerical technique used to solve the radiative transfer equation (RTE) in atmospheric and environmental physics. It is a type of Monte Carlo method that discretizes the angular and spatial domains into a set of discrete points, and then solves the RTE at each point. This method is particularly useful for solving complex problems with non-uniform media and boundary conditions.

#### 2.5a.1 The Radiative Transfer Equation

The radiative transfer equation (RTE) is a fundamental equation in atmospheric and environmental physics that describes the transfer of radiation through a medium. It is given by:

$$
\frac{1}{\rho}\frac{\partial I}{\partial x} = -\mu\frac{\partial I}{\partial x} + \frac{1}{\rho}\frac{\partial(\rho\kappa I)}{\partial x} + \frac{\partial}{\partial x}\left(\frac{1}{\rho}\frac{\partial(\rho\sigma I)}{\partial x}\right) + \frac{\partial}{\partial x}\left(\frac{1}{\rho}\frac{\partial(\rho\alpha I)}{\partial x}\right)
$$

where $I$ is the specific intensity of radiation, $\rho$ is the density of the medium, $\mu$ is the cosine of the angle of incidence, $\kappa$ is the absorption coefficient, $\sigma$ is the scattering coefficient, and $\alpha$ is the emission coefficient.

#### 2.5a.2 Discretization of the Radiative Transfer Equation

In the Discrete Ordinates Method, the RTE is discretized into a set of equations at each point in the angular and spatial domains. This is done by dividing the angular domain into a set of discrete directions, and the spatial domain into a set of discrete points. The RTE is then solved at each point, resulting in a set of equations that can be solved simultaneously.

The discretization of the RTE can be represented as:

$$
\frac{1}{\rho}\frac{\partial I}{\partial x} = -\mu\frac{\partial I}{\partial x} + \frac{1}{\rho}\frac{\partial(\rho\kappa I)}{\partial x} + \frac{\partial}{\partial x}\left(\frac{1}{\rho}\frac{\partial(\rho\sigma I)}{\partial x}\right) + \frac{\partial}{\partial x}\left(\frac{1}{\rho}\frac{\partial(\rho\alpha I)}{\partial x}\right)
$$

where $I$ is the specific intensity of radiation at a discrete point in the angular and spatial domains, $\rho$ is the density of the medium at that point, $\mu$ is the cosine of the angle of incidence at that point, $\kappa$ is the absorption coefficient at that point, $\sigma$ is the scattering coefficient at that point, and $\alpha$ is the emission coefficient at that point.

#### 2.5a.3 Solving the Discrete Ordinates Equations

The set of equations resulting from the discretization of the RTE can be solved using various numerical methods, such as the Gauss-Seidel method or the Simple Function Point method. These methods iteratively solve the equations until a desired level of accuracy is achieved.

The Discrete Ordinates Method is a powerful tool for solving complex problems in atmospheric and environmental physics. It allows for the consideration of non-uniform media and boundary conditions, and can provide detailed information about the transfer of radiation through a medium. However, it also has its limitations, such as the need for a large number of discretization points and the potential for numerical instability.




### Subsection: 2.5b Application in Radiative Transfer

The Discrete Ordinates Method (DOM) has been widely used in various applications related to radiative transfer. These applications range from atmospheric studies to remote sensing and even medical imaging. In this section, we will discuss some of the key applications of DOM in radiative transfer.

#### 2.5b.1 Atmospheric Studies

DOM has been extensively used in atmospheric studies to model the transfer of radiation through the atmosphere. This is particularly useful in understanding the Earth's energy balance and climate change. The DOM allows for the simulation of complex atmospheric conditions, including the effects of clouds, aerosols, and variations in atmospheric density. This is achieved by discretizing the angular and spatial domains into a set of equations that can be solved simultaneously.

#### 2.5b.2 Remote Sensing

DOM is also used in remote sensing, where it is used to model the transfer of radiation from the Earth's surface to a remote sensor. This is crucial in various applications, such as monitoring vegetation health, detecting changes in land use, and studying the Earth's surface temperature. The DOM allows for the simulation of different surface conditions, including the effects of vegetation, soil, and water. This is achieved by discretizing the angular and spatial domains into a set of equations that can be solved simultaneously.

#### 2.5b.3 Medical Imaging

In medical imaging, DOM is used to model the transfer of radiation through the human body. This is crucial in various imaging techniques, such as computed tomography (CT) and positron emission tomography (PET). The DOM allows for the simulation of different tissue conditions, including the effects of bone, muscle, and fat. This is achieved by discretizing the angular and spatial domains into a set of equations that can be solved simultaneously.

In conclusion, the Discrete Ordinates Method is a powerful tool for modeling radiative transfer in various applications. Its ability to handle complex media and boundary conditions makes it a valuable tool in atmospheric studies, remote sensing, and medical imaging.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fundamental concepts of absorption and emission in the context of atmospheric radiation. We have explored the physical processes that govern these phenomena, and how they interact with the Earth's atmosphere. We have also examined the mathematical models that describe these processes, and how they can be used to predict the behavior of atmospheric radiation.

We have learned that absorption is the process by which atmospheric radiation is absorbed by molecules in the atmosphere, causing them to transition to a higher energy state. This process is governed by the Beer-Lambert Law, which states that the amount of absorption is directly proportional to the concentration of the absorbing molecules and the path length of the radiation through the atmosphere.

On the other hand, emission is the process by which molecules in the atmosphere emit radiation as they transition from a higher energy state to a lower energy state. This process is governed by the Planck's Law, which states that the amount of emission is directly proportional to the temperature of the molecules and the wavelength of the emitted radiation.

We have also discussed the concept of absorption and emission coefficients, which are used to quantify the amount of absorption and emission that occurs in the atmosphere. These coefficients are crucial in understanding the behavior of atmospheric radiation, and are used in many atmospheric models.

In conclusion, absorption and emission are fundamental processes in the context of atmospheric radiation. They play a crucial role in determining the amount and distribution of radiation in the atmosphere, and are essential in understanding the Earth's climate system.

## Chapter: Molecular Absorption

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 3: Molecular Absorption. This chapter delves into the fascinating world of molecular absorption, a fundamental process in atmospheric radiation. Molecular absorption is a key component in the complex interplay of light and matter in the atmosphere, and understanding it is crucial for comprehending the broader dynamics of atmospheric radiation.

Molecular absorption is the process by which molecules in the atmosphere absorb electromagnetic radiation. This absorption is selective, with different molecules absorbing different frequencies of radiation. The absorbed energy can then be re-emitted, often at a different frequency, in a process known as emission. These processes are governed by the principles of quantum mechanics, and understanding them requires a solid grasp of concepts such as energy levels, transitions, and quantum states.

In this chapter, we will explore the principles of molecular absorption, including the quantum mechanical basis for absorption and emission. We will also discuss the role of molecular absorption in atmospheric radiation, and how it contributes to phenomena such as the greenhouse effect and the formation of the ozone layer.

We will also delve into the practical applications of molecular absorption, such as its use in remote sensing and atmospheric modeling. These applications are crucial for our understanding of the Earth's atmosphere and climate system, and they highlight the importance of molecular absorption in the broader context of atmospheric science.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to molecular absorption, equipping readers with the knowledge and tools to understand and analyze this complex process. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or simply a curious reader, we hope that this chapter will deepen your understanding of atmospheric radiation and its role in our world.

## Chapter: Molecular Absorption

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 3: Molecular Absorption. This chapter delves into the fascinating world of molecular absorption, a fundamental concept in the study of atmospheric radiation. Molecular absorption is a process that occurs when molecules in the atmosphere absorb electromagnetic radiation, causing a change in their energy state. This process plays a crucial role in the Earth's climate system, influencing the amount of solar radiation that reaches the surface and the distribution of heat in the atmosphere.

In this chapter, we will explore the principles of molecular absorption, starting with the basic concepts and gradually moving on to more complex theories and models. We will discuss the factors that influence molecular absorption, such as the wavelength of the incoming radiation, the composition of the atmosphere, and the temperature. We will also look at how molecular absorption is measured and how these measurements are used in climate research.

We will also delve into the mathematical models that describe molecular absorption. These models, expressed in terms of equations, provide a quantitative understanding of the absorption process. For instance, the Beer-Lambert Law, expressed as `$A = \epsilon \cdot c \cdot l$`, where `$A$` is the absorbance, `$\epsilon$` is the molar absorptivity, `$c$` is the concentration of the absorbing species, and `$l$` is the path length of the light through the medium, is a fundamental equation in the study of molecular absorption.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of molecular absorption and its role in atmospheric radiation. You should also be able to apply the principles and equations learned in this chapter to understand and predict the behavior of the Earth's climate system.

So, let's embark on this exciting journey into the world of molecular absorption and its role in atmospheric radiation.




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fundamental concepts of absorption and emission in the context of atmospheric radiation. We have learned that absorption is the process by which a molecule or particle absorbs a photon of electromagnetic radiation, while emission is the process by which a molecule or particle releases a photon of electromagnetic radiation. These processes play a crucial role in the transfer of energy in the atmosphere, and understanding them is essential for comprehending the complex dynamics of atmospheric radiation.

We have also delved into the mathematical models that describe these processes. The absorption coefficient, denoted by `$\alpha(\nu)$`, is a measure of the amount of radiation that is absorbed per unit path length. The emission coefficient, denoted by `$\beta(\nu)$`, is a measure of the amount of radiation that is emitted per unit volume per unit time. These coefficients are crucial for understanding the behavior of radiation in the atmosphere.

Furthermore, we have explored the concept of the Planck function, which describes the spectral density of electromagnetic radiation emitted by a black body. The Planck function is given by the equation `$$
B(\nu, T) = \frac{2h\nu^3}{c^2} \frac{1}{e^{h\nu/kT} - 1}
$$`, where `$h$` is Planck's constant, `$\nu$` is the frequency of the radiation, `$c$` is the speed of light, `$k$` is Boltzmann's constant, and `$T$` is the temperature of the black body.

In conclusion, absorption and emission are fundamental processes in the transfer of energy in the atmosphere. Understanding these processes and their mathematical descriptions is crucial for comprehending the complex dynamics of atmospheric radiation.

#### Exercise 3

In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the topic of absorption and emission, focusing on the concept of scattering. We will also discuss the mathematical models that describe these processes, including the Rayleigh scattering formula and the Mie scattering formula. Furthermore, we have explored the concept of the Planck function, which describes the spectral density of electromagnetic radiation emitted by a black body. The Planck function is given by the equation `$$
B(\nu, T) = \frac{2h\nu^3}{c^2} \frac{1}{e^{h\nu/kT} - 1}
$$`, where `$h$` is Planck's constant, `$\nu$` is the frequency of the radiation, `$c$` is the speed of light, `$k$` is Boltzmann's constant, and `$T$` is the temperature of the black body.

In conclusion, absorption and emission are fundamental processes in the transfer of energy in the atmosphere. Understanding these processes and their mathematical descriptions is crucial for comprehending the complex dynamics of atmospheric radiation.

### Exercises

#### Exercise 2

## Chapter: Atmospheric Radiation: A Comprehensive Guide

### Introduction

In the previous chapter, we discussed the fundamental concepts of absorption and emission in the context of atmospheric radiation. We learned that absorption is the process by which a molecule or particle in the atmosphere absorbs a photon of electromagnetic radiation, while emission is the process by which a molecule or particle releases a photon of electromagnetic radiation. These processes play a crucial role in the transfer of energy in the atmosphere, and understanding them is essential for comprehending the complex dynamics of atmospheric radiation.

In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the topic of absorption and emission, focusing on the concept of scattering. Scattering is a fundamental process in atmospheric radiation, and it plays a crucial role in determining the amount and distribution of radiation in the atmosphere. We will explore the different types of scattering, including Rayleigh scattering, Mie scattering, and Raman scattering, and discuss their respective roles in the atmosphere.

We will also discuss the mathematical models that describe these processes, including the Rayleigh scattering formula and the Mie scattering formula. These models are essential tools for understanding and predicting the behavior of atmospheric radiation, and they are widely used in various fields, including meteorology, climatology, and environmental science.

Finally, we will explore the practical applications of scattering in atmospheric radiation, including its role in determining the color of the sky, the formation of rainbows, and the scattering of sunlight in the atmosphere. By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of scattering and its importance in the study of atmospheric radiation. 


## Chapter 3: Scattering:




### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 3 of "Atmospheric Radiation: A Comprehensive Guide". In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of radiation scattering in the atmosphere. This is a crucial aspect of atmospheric science, as it plays a significant role in the distribution of solar radiation and the formation of weather patterns.

Radiation scattering is the process by which light or other electromagnetic radiation is redirected from its original path due to interaction with particles or molecules in the atmosphere. This interaction can occur with various atmospheric constituents, including water vapor, dust, and pollutants. The scattering of radiation can have profound effects on the Earth's climate and weather patterns, making it a topic of great interest to scientists and researchers.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental principles of radiation scattering, including the different types of scattering and the factors that influence them. We will also discuss the mathematical models used to describe scattering, such as the Rayleigh scattering formula and the Mie scattering formula. These models are essential tools for understanding and predicting the behavior of radiation in the atmosphere.

We will also delve into the practical applications of radiation scattering, such as its role in remote sensing and climate modeling. By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of radiation scattering and its importance in atmospheric science. So, let's embark on this exciting journey together and discover the wonders of radiation scattering in the atmosphere.




### Section: 3.1 Scattering by Molecules and Particles:

Scattering of radiation is a fundamental process in atmospheric science, as it plays a crucial role in the distribution of solar radiation and the formation of weather patterns. In this section, we will explore the scattering of radiation by molecules and particles, which is a key component of the overall scattering process.

#### 3.1a Rayleigh Scattering

Rayleigh scattering is a type of scattering that occurs when light interacts with molecules or particles that are much smaller than the wavelength of the light. This type of scattering is named after the British physicist Lord Rayleigh, who first derived the mathematical formula for Rayleigh scattering in 1871.

The intensity of Rayleigh scattered radiation, denoted as $I$, is given by the equation:

$$
I = \frac{I_0}{R^2} \cdot \frac{d^2 \cdot \pi \cdot n^2 \cdot \lambda^4 \cdot P(cos(\theta))}{64 \cdot \pi^2 \cdot c^4 \cdot \tau^2}
$$

where $I_0$ is the intensity of the incident light, $R$ is the distance between the scattering molecule and the observer, $d$ is the diameter of the scattering molecule, $n$ is the refractive index of the molecule, $\lambda$ is the wavelength of the incident light, $P(cos(\theta))$ is the scattering phase function, $c$ is the speed of light, and $\tau$ is the optical depth.

The Rayleigh scattering formula shows that the intensity of scattered radiation is directly proportional to the fourth power of the wavelength, which explains why the sky appears blue. The shorter blue wavelengths are scattered more than the longer red wavelengths, making the sky appear blue.

Rayleigh scattering is also responsible for the twinkling of stars. As light from a star passes through the Earth's atmosphere, it is scattered by molecules and particles, causing the star to appear to twinkle. This effect is more pronounced for smaller stars, as they have a larger proportion of their light scattered by the atmosphere.

In addition to its effects on the appearance of the sky and stars, Rayleigh scattering also plays a crucial role in the distribution of solar radiation in the atmosphere. As sunlight enters the atmosphere, it is scattered by molecules and particles, causing it to spread out and lose intensity. This scattering process is responsible for the gradual decrease in sunlight intensity as it travels through the atmosphere.

In the next section, we will explore another type of scattering, Mie scattering, which occurs when light interacts with particles that are larger than the wavelength of the light.

#### 3.1b Mie Scattering

Mie scattering is another type of scattering that occurs when light interacts with particles that are larger than the wavelength of the light. This type of scattering is named after the German physicist Gustav Mie, who first derived the mathematical formula for Mie scattering in 1908.

The intensity of Mie scattered radiation, denoted as $I$, is given by the equation:

$$
I = \frac{I_0}{R^2} \cdot \frac{d^2 \cdot \pi \cdot n^2 \cdot \lambda^4 \cdot P(cos(\theta))}{64 \cdot \pi^2 \cdot c^4 \cdot \tau^2}
$$

where $I_0$ is the intensity of the incident light, $R$ is the distance between the scattering particle and the observer, $d$ is the diameter of the scattering particle, $n$ is the refractive index of the particle, $\lambda$ is the wavelength of the incident light, $P(cos(\theta))$ is the scattering phase function, $c$ is the speed of light, and $\tau$ is the optical depth.

Unlike Rayleigh scattering, Mie scattering is not dependent on the wavelength of the incident light. This means that all wavelengths of light are scattered equally, resulting in a white appearance when viewed from a distance. This is why clouds appear white, as they are composed of water droplets that are large enough to scatter light through Mie scattering.

Mie scattering also plays a crucial role in the distribution of solar radiation in the atmosphere. As sunlight enters the atmosphere, it is scattered by molecules and particles, causing it to spread out and lose intensity. However, Mie scattering is more efficient at scattering light than Rayleigh scattering, meaning that more of the incident light is scattered back into space. This is why the sky appears brighter when viewed through a thick layer of clouds, as the clouds are scattering more of the incident light back into space.

In the next section, we will explore the effects of scattering on the Earth's climate and weather patterns.

#### 3.1c Scattering by Aerosols

Aerosols are a crucial component of the Earth's atmosphere, playing a significant role in the scattering of radiation. Aerosols are defined as a suspension of solid or liquid particles in a gas, with the particles typically ranging in size from 0.001 micrometers to 100 micrometers. These particles can be either natural, such as dust and sea salt, or anthropogenic, such as pollution from industrial activities.

The scattering of radiation by aerosols is a complex process that involves both Rayleigh and Mie scattering. The size of the aerosol particles determines which type of scattering occurs. Particles smaller than 0.1 micrometers typically scatter radiation through Rayleigh scattering, while larger particles scatter radiation through Mie scattering.

The scattering of radiation by aerosols has significant implications for the Earth's climate and weather patterns. Aerosols can directly affect the amount of solar radiation reaching the Earth's surface by scattering it back into space. This can have a cooling effect on the Earth's climate, particularly in regions where aerosols are abundant.

Aerosols also play a crucial role in the formation of clouds. As aerosols rise into the atmosphere, they can act as nuclei for the condensation of water vapor, forming clouds. The type of aerosol can also influence the type of cloud that forms. For example, pollution aerosols can lead to the formation of low-lying, dark clouds, which can have a warming effect on the Earth's climate.

The scattering of radiation by aerosols can also have indirect effects on the Earth's climate. For instance, aerosols can alter the amount of sunlight reaching the Earth's surface, which can affect the growth of plants and other vegetation. This, in turn, can impact the carbon cycle, which plays a crucial role in regulating the Earth's climate.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the effects of scattering on the Earth's climate and weather patterns, focusing on the role of aerosols in these processes.

#### 3.1d Scattering by Clouds

Clouds are another significant component of the Earth's atmosphere that contribute to the scattering of radiation. They are composed of tiny water droplets or ice crystals suspended in the atmosphere. The size of these droplets or crystals can range from a few micrometers to several millimeters, making them ideal for Mie scattering.

The scattering of radiation by clouds is a complex process that involves both Mie scattering and reflection. Mie scattering occurs when the incident radiation is scattered in all directions, while reflection occurs when the incident radiation is returned in the opposite direction. The amount of scattering and reflection depends on the size and composition of the cloud particles, as well as the wavelength of the incident radiation.

Clouds can have a significant impact on the Earth's climate and weather patterns. They can reflect incoming solar radiation back into space, reducing the amount of radiation reaching the Earth's surface and cooling the planet. This effect is particularly pronounced for high, thin cirrus clouds, which can reflect up to 70% of incoming solar radiation.

On the other hand, low-lying, thick clouds can have a warming effect on the Earth's climate. These clouds can absorb incoming solar radiation, trapping it in the atmosphere and warming the planet. This effect is particularly pronounced for deep, convective clouds, which can absorb up to 70% of incoming solar radiation.

The scattering of radiation by clouds can also have indirect effects on the Earth's climate. For instance, clouds can alter the amount of sunlight reaching the Earth's surface, which can affect the growth of plants and other vegetation. This, in turn, can impact the carbon cycle, which plays a crucial role in regulating the Earth's climate.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the effects of scattering on the Earth's climate and weather patterns, focusing on the role of clouds in these processes.

#### 3.1e Scattering by Ice and Snow

Ice and snow are two more important components of the Earth's atmosphere that contribute to the scattering of radiation. They are composed of ice crystals, which can be either transparent or opaque, depending on their size and orientation. The scattering of radiation by ice and snow is a complex process that involves both Mie scattering and reflection, similar to clouds.

The scattering of radiation by ice and snow can have significant implications for the Earth's climate and weather patterns. Ice and snow can reflect incoming solar radiation back into space, reducing the amount of radiation reaching the Earth's surface and cooling the planet. This effect is particularly pronounced for transparent ice crystals, which can reflect up to 80% of incoming solar radiation.

On the other hand, opaque ice crystals can have a warming effect on the Earth's climate. These crystals can absorb incoming solar radiation, trapping it in the atmosphere and warming the planet. This effect is particularly pronounced for deep, convective ice crystals, which can absorb up to 60% of incoming solar radiation.

The scattering of radiation by ice and snow can also have indirect effects on the Earth's climate. For instance, ice and snow can alter the albedo of the Earth's surface, which can affect the amount of sunlight reaching the Earth's surface. This, in turn, can impact the growth of plants and other vegetation, as well as the carbon cycle.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the effects of scattering on the Earth's climate and weather patterns, focusing on the role of ice and snow in these processes.

#### 3.1f Scattering by Water

Water is a crucial component of the Earth's atmosphere, playing a significant role in the scattering of radiation. It is composed of water molecules, which can be either transparent or opaque, depending on their size and orientation. The scattering of radiation by water is a complex process that involves both Mie scattering and reflection, similar to ice and snow.

The scattering of radiation by water can have significant implications for the Earth's climate and weather patterns. Water can reflect incoming solar radiation back into space, reducing the amount of radiation reaching the Earth's surface and cooling the planet. This effect is particularly pronounced for transparent water molecules, which can reflect up to 70% of incoming solar radiation.

On the other hand, opaque water molecules can have a warming effect on the Earth's climate. These molecules can absorb incoming solar radiation, trapping it in the atmosphere and warming the planet. This effect is particularly pronounced for deep, convective water molecules, which can absorb up to 50% of incoming solar radiation.

The scattering of radiation by water can also have indirect effects on the Earth's climate. For instance, water can alter the albedo of the Earth's surface, which can affect the amount of sunlight reaching the Earth's surface. This, in turn, can impact the growth of plants and other vegetation, as well as the carbon cycle.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the effects of scattering on the Earth's climate and weather patterns, focusing on the role of water in these processes.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of atmospheric radiation, specifically focusing on the scattering of radiation. We have explored the fundamental principles that govern the scattering of radiation, including the Rayleigh scattering and Mie scattering models. These models provide a mathematical framework for understanding how radiation interacts with molecules and particles in the atmosphere, and how this interaction affects the distribution of radiation in the atmosphere.

We have also discussed the practical implications of scattering, including its role in the formation of clouds and the color of the sky. The scattering of radiation by molecules and particles in the atmosphere is responsible for the blue color of the sky, a phenomenon that has fascinated scientists and philosophers for centuries.

Finally, we have touched upon the importance of understanding scattering in the context of climate change. The scattering of radiation plays a crucial role in the Earth's energy balance, and any changes in this process can have significant implications for the Earth's climate.

In conclusion, the scattering of radiation is a fundamental aspect of atmospheric science. It is a complex process that is governed by a set of mathematical models, and it has profound implications for the Earth's climate and weather patterns. By understanding the principles of scattering, we can gain a deeper understanding of the workings of the atmosphere and contribute to the development of solutions for some of the most pressing environmental challenges of our time.

## Chapter 4: Absorption

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 4: Absorption, a crucial component of our comprehensive guide on atmospheric radiation. This chapter will delve into the fascinating world of absorption, a fundamental process that plays a significant role in the interaction of radiation with the Earth's atmosphere.

Absorption is one of the three primary processes that radiation undergoes when it interacts with the atmosphere, the other two being reflection and transmission. It is a process by which an absorbing medium absorbs a portion of the radiation passing through it, converting it into heat. This process is governed by the Beer-Lambert Law, which states that the amount of absorption is directly proportional to the concentration of the absorbing species and the path length of the radiation through the medium.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical models that describe absorption, such as the equation for absorption coefficient, denoted as $\alpha(\lambda)$. This equation is given by:

$$
\alpha(\lambda) = N_a \sigma(\lambda)
$$

where $N_a$ is the number density of the absorbing species and $\sigma(\lambda)$ is the absorption cross-section.

We will also discuss the implications of absorption on the Earth's climate and weather patterns. The absorption of solar radiation by the atmosphere is a key factor in determining the Earth's temperature and the distribution of heat in the atmosphere. This process is also crucial in the formation of clouds and precipitation.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of the process of absorption, its mathematical description, and its role in atmospheric radiation. This knowledge will serve as a foundation for the subsequent chapters, where we will delve deeper into the complex interplay of radiation with the Earth's atmosphere.

So, let's embark on this exciting journey into the world of absorption, a world where light is absorbed, heat is generated, and the Earth's climate and weather patterns are shaped.

## Approximations

### Rayleigh approximation (scattering)

Rayleigh scattering describes the elastic scattering of light by spheres that are much smaller than the wavelength of light. The intensity "I" of the scattered radiation is given by
where "I<sub>0</sub>" is the light intensity before the interaction with the particle, "R" is the distance between the particle and the observer, "θ" is the scattering angle, "λ" is the wavelength of light under consideration, "n" is the refractive index of the particle, and "d" is the diameter of the particle.

It can be seen from the above equation that Rayleigh scattering is strongly dependent upon the size of the particle and the wavelengths. The intensity of the Rayleigh scattered radiation increases rapidly as the ratio of particle size to wavelength increases. Furthermore, the intensity of Rayleigh scattered radiation is identical in the forward and reverse directions.

The Rayleigh scattering model breaks down when the particle size becomes larger than around 10% of the wavelength of the incident radiation. In the case of particles with dimensions greater than this, Mie's scattering model can be used to find the intensity of the scattered radiation. The intensity of Mie scattered radiation is given by the summation of an infinite series of terms rather than by a simple mathematical expression. It can be shown, however, that scattering in this range of particle sizes differs from Rayleigh scattering in several respects: it is roughly independent of wavelength and it is larger in the forward direction than in the reverse direction. The greater the particle size, the more of the light is scattered in the forward direction.

The blue colour of the sky results from Rayleigh scattering, as the size of the gas particles in the atmosphere is much smaller than the wavelength of visible light. Rayleigh scattering is much greater for blue light than for red light, which is why the sky appears blue.
